                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3v1.5%E2%88%92xcr0.5%2Bx3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3V1.5%E2%88%92xCr0.5%2Bx%28PO4%293">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5−<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Structural+Analysis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_structural%20analysis">structural analysis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Density+Functional+Theory&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_density%20functional%20theory">density functional theory</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrochemical+Properties&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrochemical%20properties">electrochemical properties</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) play an important part in the modern society, and they are deemed as the prospect of technology for energy storage systems and electric vehicles. Meanwhile, the ever-increasing market requirement elevates the cost of LIBs and brings about enormous challenges on the reservation of lithium element resources on earth. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Therefore, it is urgent to pursue a promising candidate as an alternative to LIBs, where sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are being intensively investigated on account of the natural abundance and relative popular prices of sodium. It is well accepted that Na/Na<sup>+</sup> is 0.3 V higher than Li/Li<sup>+</sup> versus the standard hydrogen electrode; consequently, SIBs deliver a relative lower energy density than LIBs with the same electroactive transitional metal. Therefore, it is particularly important to seek for redox couples which can operate at high potential to push up the energy density of SIBs, such as layered transition-metal oxides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 to 6">(3−6)</a> However, a series of phase transitions induced by the extraction of Na ions upon charging inevitably lead to undesirable cycling instability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 to 9">(7−9)</a> On the contrary, sodium super ionic conductors (NASICONs), for instance, such as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with an average operation potential of 3.4 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, related with the redox couple of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, have been well documented as a cathode for SIBs owing to its robust structure stability. To achieve even higher working plateaus, activating the redox couple of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> (4.1 V) has been achieved through engineering of element doping and solid solution in various material systems, including Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub><i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Al<sub><i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>VCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and so forth. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 10 to 15">(10−15)</a> However, taking Na<sub>3</sub>VCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as an example, the activation of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> can lead to 1.5-electron transfer, increasing the energy density to some extent, but meanwhile brings in irreversible phase change and causes significant capacity decay upon cycling. A comprehensive understanding of the influence of Cr<sup>3+</sup> replacement on the crystal structure and electrochemical property is pretty necessary and vital, when accessing the reversible V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple. Therefore, it is essential to achieve a balance between achieving high specific capacity and maintaining long-life cycling performance in this material system.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with different V/Cr ratios was synthesized via sol–gel method, and among them, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits the best electrochemical properties. Electrochemical performance and ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis indicated that both solid solution and two-phase reactions attend the cycling process of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode. Using the structural analysis, electrochemical measurements, density functional theory calculation, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and cyclic voltammetry, the comprehensive influence of V<sup>3+</sup> by Cr<sup>3+</sup> substitution was investigated.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">A typical sol–gel method was employed to synthesize Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5). All chemical reagents were utilized as received without further purification. Typically, as for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, 4 mmol anhydrous citric acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, 99.5%) was dissolved into deionized water with constant stirring. After that, 6 mmol sodium acetate (CH<sub>3</sub>COONa, 99.0%), 3 mmol vanadium (III) 2, 4-pentanedionate (C<sub>15</sub>H<sub>21</sub>O<sub>6</sub>V, 97.0%), 1 mmol chromic nitrate nonahydrate [Cr(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, 99.0%], and 6 mmol ammonium dihydrogen phosphate (NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, 99.0%) were added into solution. The solution was kept stirring at 100 °C. After the water evaporated, a sol was achieved, which was further put into an oven at 120 °C for 12 h to get a gel. Finally, the as-obtained gel was sintered at 800 °C for 12 h in Ar. Regarding the other kinds of sample, the same synthesis method was utilized by merely altering the stoichiometric amount of C<sub>15</sub>H<sub>21</sub>O<sub>6</sub>V and Cr(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O.</div><div class="NLM_p">The phase structure of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was identified using Smart Lab outfitted with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 0.1541 nm). Rietveld refinement of the XRD patterns was conducted with a GSAS code. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The button batteries were stopped at assigned voltage and separated in an Ar-filled glove box for ex situ XRD analysis. The microstructure was investigated using SEM (scanning electron microscopy) and TEM (transmission electron microscopy). The chemical states of V and Cr were determined with X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo ESCALAB 250XI). Carbon content in the sample was identified by thermogravimetric analysis (TG) tests.</div><div class="NLM_p">The positive electrodes were prepared via mixing 10 wt % super P, 10 wt % PVDF (polyvinylidene fluoride), and 80 wt % of the active materials. NMP (1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone) was used as the solvent to make slurry, which is carefully coated onto Al foil and dried at 60 °C for 12 h. Na metal and glass fiber filters were used as counter electrode and separator, respectively. The liquid electrolyte was made of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate. All of them were fitted together with CR2032 button batteries. Galvanostatic charge–discharge, rate performance, and long cycling capability measurements were conducted on a Land Battery Test System (CT2001A). EIS analysis was performed by employing an electrochemistry workstation (CHI600E, China). For the full cell, the negative electrode was prepared by mixing the PVDF (10 wt %), hard carbon (80 wt %), and super P (10 wt %). The synthesis of hard carbon was by pyrolysis of zinc citrate and followed by washing with dilute hydrochloric acid. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> The slurry was made by adding an appropriate amount of NMP, which is then pasted onto Cu foil. The mass loadings of the active material were 2.5–3.2 and 2.0–2.7 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and hard carbon, respectively. The full cell balance was realized by adjusting the electrode capacity ratio of the cathode and anode to 0.83. The capacity of the full cell was figured out based on the mass of the cathode active material.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The vacancy of Na and the order of V and Cr were implemented using the GULP code. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The Buckingham potentials were employed to model non-bonded interactions. Geometric relaxation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) was computed by the VASP (Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package) with the PBE (Perdew–Burke–Ernzerh) of exchange–correlation functional and the PAW (projector augmented waves), covered spin polarization, and the plane-wave basis was cut off by 520 eV. All atoms used were a 2 × 2 × 1 gamma grid and relaxed by conjugated gradient method, until the maximal force was less than 0.05 eV Å<sup>–1</sup> on each atom and the energy convergence condition was 10<sup>–4</sup> eV. The Hubbard type U correction of vanadium atom and chromic atom were 4.2 and 3.7 eV for 3d electrons, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> The calculated crystal structures were visualized using the VESTA program. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Sodium ion diffusion barriers were computed by using the nudged elastic band method.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The XRD patterns of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, and all of these synthesized materials are consistent well with the NASICON structure. The crystal structure of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was also revisited through using the structure of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a starting structural model. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The whole XRD patterns are well indexed with the rhombohedral <i>R</i>-3<i>c</i> (167) space group. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S3</a> showed the Rietveld refinements and atomic coordinates of these samples. Cell parameters and agreement factors are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>. On the basis of the Rietveld refinement, the lattice parameters of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.7050 (2) Å, <i>c</i> = 21.8166 (6) Å, and <i>V</i> = 1431.7 Å<sup>3</sup>; the lattice parameters of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.6917 (5) Å, <i>c</i> = 21.8473 (16) Å, and <i>V</i> = 1429.4 Å<sup>3</sup>; and the lattice parameters of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.0</sub>Cr<sub>1.0</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 8.669 (1) Å, <i>c</i> = 21.768 (3) Å, and <i>V</i> = 1416.7 Å<sup>3</sup>. Two kinds of sodium ion [Na (1) and Na (2)] are separately located on the (0, 0, 0) (6b, six fold coordination) and (<i>x</i>, 0, 0.25) (18e, eight fold coordination) Wyckoff sites in the crystal structure. It indicated that the experimental occupancy factors of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> for Na (1) and Na (2) are 0.863 and 0.716, respectively, corresponding to the number of Na (1) = 6 × 0.863 = 5.2 and the number of Na (2) = 18 × 0.716 = 12.9 in per unit cell. From the experimental occupancy factors of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.0</sub>Cr<sub>1.0</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, a similar result has been achieved. The cell parameters of the other compositions are also illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, and it indicated that lattice parameter <i>a</i> and the cell volume showed a decreased trend with increasing the Cr content, which is assigned for the smaller radius of Cr<sup>3+</sup> (0.755 Å) with respect to V<sup>3+</sup> (0.780 Å). Moreover, lattice parameter <i>c</i> does not strictly follow a downward trend with Cr increasing. Also, this behavior can be interpreted by the small rotations of V/CrO<sub>6</sub> and PO<sub>4</sub> around the three- and two-fold axis, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. (b) XRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. (c) Lattice parameters and lattice volume variations with different compositions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11975&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and microstructure feature of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was researched using SEM and TEM. As observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,b, the typical product was made up of particles with the size ranging from several to tens of micrometer. Moreover, energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping was used to determine the composition of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c indicated that Na, V, Cr, P, O, and C elements are homogeneously distributed. The high-resolution transition electron microscopy TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> clearly exhibited that the crystallized particles were coated by carbon shell with a thickness of 3 nm. 0.62 nm <i>d</i>-spacing of lattice fringe can be clearly indexed, matching (012) plane of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. According to the TG curve plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e, the relative weight ratio of carbon was 7.6% for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a,b) SEM images and (c) EDS mapping of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub>. (d) HR-TEM image of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub>. (e) TG curve of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11975&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical state was identified via XPS. The spectrum shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> revealed the coexistence of C, Na, V, Cr, P, and O elements in the sample. The chemical states of V and Cr are determined by core-level spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The V 2p core-level spectra consists of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (516.7 eV) and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (523.5 eV), illustrating the trivalent state of V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> The Cr 2p core-level spectra consist of Cr 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (577.9 eV) and Cr 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (587.1 eV), demonstrating the trivalent state of Cr. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a depicted the charge–discharge curves within the voltage range of 2.5–4.4 V at 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Observably, two distinct potential plateau located at 3.4 and 4.1 V can be observed, corresponding to the redox couples of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>, respectively. Moreover, the specific capacity contributed by the redox couples of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> varied with the content of Cr<sup>3+</sup>. Under the lower content of Cr<sup>3+</sup>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.1, and 0.2) can deliver a similar specific capacity, and the increasing specific capacity at V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> plateau can basically make up for the decreasing specific capacity at V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> plateau. However, for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, the increased specific capacity of the high potential platform cannot compensate for the reduced specific capacity of the low potential platform, finally leading to a capacity decrease. In addition, the relative low initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> can be ascribed to the decomposition of liquid electrolyte at high potential and the formation of the SEI (solid electrolyte interlayer). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. (a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 2.5–4.4 V. (b) Discharge capacity as a function of cycle number at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 2.5–4.4 V. (c) Rate capacity at different current densities. (d) Long-term cycling performance at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11975&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the rate capability of the samples at different current densities, the rate properties were presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. It is evident that Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits the best rate capability, and the discharge capacities are 77.0, 76.3, 73.1, 67.6, 61.0, and 49.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. As the current density reversed back to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a discharge specific capacity of 75.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can be restored, signifying the excellent reversibility. On other hand, appealing long term cycling capability is also achieved with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. After 500 cycles, the capacity retention is 77.6% with an average CE of about 98.5% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). Based on the point of charge compensation mechanism, herein, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is the most promising cathode material.</div><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ XRD analysis was carried out to further probe the structure transition of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrodes upon cycling. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a exhibited the charge/discharge profiles and the selected states of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode for the ex situ XRD analysis. Observably, the variations of the diffraction peak position with the voltage are dramatic. The evolution of these diffraction peaks [such as (104), (113), (024), (211), (116), and (300)] is highly reversible. Despite the limited resolution of the used X-ray diffractometer, a solid solution reaction mechanism can be apparently observed on account of the continuous peak shift. Specifically, when charged from 4.1 to 4.4 V, (006) and (211) peaks disappear and then gradually appear again when discharged to 4.1 V; (110), (113), (024), (116), and (300) peaks obviously shift toward a high angle and become wider. It is considered that both solid-solution and two-phase reactions are involved in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode during charge and discharge process. The charge/discharge curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) can reconfirm this conclusion. It is generally recognized that the oblique line part represents a solid solution reaction, and two flat plateaus signify a two-phase reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Ex situ XRD patterns of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during the first charge/discharge process at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Ex situ XRD patterns of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. XPS spectra of (c) V 2p and (d) Cr 2p in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>Cr<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> under different charging states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11975&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b showed the XRD patterns of fresh Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) and after 100th cycle. As depicted, the diffraction peaks cannot completely reverse back to their pristine location after 100 cycles, displaying a non-reversible cell volume change with respect to the pristine; the diffraction peaks after 100th cycle shifted toward a higher angle, suggesting the decease of cell parameters. Based on the shift amplitude of the peak position, it was evident that the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode possesses the smallest shift, implying a minimum structural change and an excellent stability during the cycling process. The sodium storage mechanism of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was further investigated through ex situ XPS analysis. Upon charging to 3.6 V, the peak of V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> shifted to 523.96 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), signifying the appearance of V<sup>4+</sup>. When charging to 4.4 V, the binding energy of V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> was about 524.07 eV, implying the formation of a small amount of V<sup>5+</sup>. In contrast, the binding energies of Cr 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Cr 2p<sub>1/2</sub> kept unchanged during charge process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d).</div><div class="NLM_p">It is known that comprehension of the sodium ion migration mechanism is of significant importance for the further promotion of battery performance. As a result, the sodium diffusion trajectories in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) were investigated by first principles calculation. Regarding Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, it is recognized that Na (1) (6b) and Na (2) (18e) positions are not fully occupied. Based on previous analysis of the crystal structure, Na (1) and Na (2) positions are occupied with 5 and 13 sodium atoms, respectively. For the sodium order of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, there are 51408 possibilities. Using the VASP to find a rational structure is pretty expensive and uneconomic. Therefore, in this work, we used atomistic simulation methods based on force field methods, GULP, to search for a rational structure. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) was considered as an initial crystal structure, taking into account van der Waals interactions, a short-range component, and a long-range Coulombic force. An additional three-body term, covalent forces are also important, which take into account the rigidity of O–P–O bonds and angle dependent nature for the PO<sub>4</sub> units. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> To provide a simple means to involve the effect of electronic polarization, we adopted the shell model for O<sup>2–</sup>, V<sup>3+</sup>, and Cr<sup>3+</sup> ions which allows the polarization effects of charged defects and has been testified available for a wide range of oxide-based ceramics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The structure of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was optimized under a constant pressure of 0 Pa, and ion positions and lattice parameters were permitted to relax. In addition, the detailed parameters are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Suitable sodium ion occupied sites were calculated through the lattice parameters of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The order of TM (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) have 220, 495, and 924 possibilities, respectively. The order of V and Cr was calculated using the lattice parameters provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S3</a>. In current research, the path of the Na (1) site to the nearest Na (2) site was researched since this path is most reasonable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The sodium ions diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) at room temperature can be calculated according to the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, <i>a</i> is the constant associated with crystal structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>), Γ is individual defect jump rates, υ is a vibrational prefactor (10<sup>11</sup> Hz), <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is the activation barrier, <i>k</i><sub>B</sub><i>T</i> is 26 meV (<i>T</i> = 300 K), <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, and <i>k</i><sub>B</sub> is Boltzmann constant. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> The values of <i>a</i>, the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>), and the results of diffusion path of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>. The diffusion path and the sodium ion diffusion energy curve are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b. The activation energy of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) was identified to be 0.239, 0.323, and 0.369 eV, respectively. The increasing content of Cr promotes the diffusion barrier and consequently slows down the diffusion kinetic of sodium ion. According to the equation, the diffusion coefficients <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) at room temperature were 1.10 × 10<sup>–8</sup>, 4.52 × 10<sup>–10</sup>, and 7.53 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In LISICON-structured materials with the composition of LiM<sub>1</sub>M<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M<sub>1</sub>, M<sub>2</sub> = Ge, Ti, Sn, Hf), the activation barrier for Li<sup>+</sup> movement along the conduction channel is closely related to the size of bottleneck between Li (1) and Li (2) site, and the larger bottleneck size implies a smaller activation energy within a certain range. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Similarly, herein, it is deemed that the bigger bottleneck between Na (1) and Na (2) sites means lower activation energy. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the Na (1) site is surrounded with six oxygen atoms in an antiprism. In the NASICON structure, Na<sup>+</sup> moves along the conduction channel between Na (1) and Na (2) sites, and Na<sup>+</sup> would move through the lateral faces of the antiprism which is termed as the bottleneck. The circumradius of the lateral faces is acknowledged as a measure of the bottleneck size. In accordance with the Rietveld refinement results of XRD pattern, the bottleneck size of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (2.271 Å) was bigger than those of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (2.232 Å) and Na<sub>3</sub>VCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (2.230 Å).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Sodium migration path in the lattice of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) bulk along the Na (1)—Na (2). (b) Energy curve along the Na (1)—Na (2) of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5). (c) Environment of the Na (1) site, and bottlenecks between Na (1) and Na (2) sites; EIS of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) before cycle (d) and after 50 cycles (e) at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11975&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">EIS analysis was carried out to validate the accuracy of the theoretical calculation. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, all of these plots were made up of a semicircle in the high frequency region and a straight line in the low frequency region. The equivalent circuit model presented in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d was applied to obtain impedance data. <i>R</i><sub>s</sub>, CPE, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, and <i>W</i><sub>o</sub> are the electrolyte resistance, the capacity of the double-layer capacitance and the surface layer, the charge-transfer resistance, and the Warburg impedance, respectively. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode exhibited the lowest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (29.44 Ω) among Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5). This result revealed that the further replacement of V by Cr would reduce the electron conductivity. Besides, on the basis of the Warburg impedances at the range of low-frequency, the sodium ions diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can be calculated. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 36 to 39">(36−39)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a> displayed the relationship between ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low frequency region and <i>Z</i>’. The values of σ and <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) are also provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S7</a>. <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (7.74 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is bigger than the others, suggesting that the further substitution of V by Cr would worsen the diffusion and kinetic of sodium ions. By comparing the EIS results with the theoretical calculation, it indicates that the accuracy of the theoretical calculation methods is satisfactory. However, the result of sodium ion kinetic cannot completely elucidate the remarkable cycling stability of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p">The EIS of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) after 50 cycles shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5b</a> displayed the relationship between ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in low-frequency range and <i>Z</i>’. The corresponding fitting results of sodium diffusion are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S8</a>, and it indicated that the Warburg factor (σ) values of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.2, and 0.5) were 21.33, 3.19, and 5.81 after 50 cycles, respectively. When compared with <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S7</a>, the diffusion coefficient of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode was significantly decreased after 50 cycles. In contrast, the diffusion coefficient of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode varied little after 50 cycles. Associated with ex situ XRD analysis, it is deduced that the small volume changes of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode upon the cycling is responsible for the stable channel of sodium ion mobility, finally inducing the best cycling stability.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> showed the CV (cyclic voltammetry) curves of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at various scan rates, and all of these curves possess two couples of cathodic/anodic peaks. The slope value can be utilized to roughly compare the diffusion coefficient at different charge/discharge states via the following equation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>I</i><sub>p</sub>, <i>S</i>, <i>n</i>, υ, and Δ<i>C</i><sub>0</sub> are the peak current, the surface area of electrode, the number of electrons per species reaction, the scan rate, and the sodium ion concentration, respectively. Based on the slope values, compared with discharge process, the diffusion coefficient is faster during charge process; compared with high plateau, the diffusion coefficient of low plateau is faster.</div><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of above results, the full cell illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a was constructed by utilizing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon as the cathode and anode, respectively. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>, the XRD pattern of hard carbon displays two broad diffraction peaks approximately located at 2θ ≈ 22.4 and 43.4°, corresponding to (002) and (101) planes of the graphite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> The initial charge specific capacity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/suppl_file/am0c11975_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>) was 107.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and there remained 94.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 70 cycles, suggesting an excellent cycling performance. The full cell of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon was assembled by coupling the potential plateau of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and hard carbon. The charge/discharge profiles of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon full cell are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. These two potential plateaus of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode are associated with the redox reaction of V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>, and a sloping voltage profile of hard carbon is related to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into disordered hard carbon interlayers. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c presents the charge/discharge curves of the first three cycles for the full cell cycled in the region of 0.5–4.4 V, and because of the formation of the SEI on the surface of hard carbon, an evident irreversible capacity was observed in the initial cycle, demonstrating a CE of 27.01%. As indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, the discharge capacity of the first and the 50th cycle are 84.3 and 56.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and the average CE is about 92.7%.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/medium/am0c11975_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Schematic diagram of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon full cell. (b) Voltage profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and hard carbon half cells and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon full cell. (c) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon full cell at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.5–4.4 V. (d) Cycling performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//hard carbon full cell at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.5–4.4 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11975/asset/images/large/am0c11975_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11975&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To sum up, by optimizing the content of Cr, a solid state solution of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5–<i>x</i></sub>Cr<sub>0.5+<i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was constructed. The introduction of Cr<sup>3+</sup> can induce the redox reaction of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>, rendering a new strategy for improving energy density of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. Taking a full account of electrochemistry properties, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was the best choice; the initial discharge capacity of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.3</sub>Cr<sub>0.7</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was 78.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and the highly stable structure rendered a well capacity retention (the discharge capacity was 67 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycle). Ex situ XRD, density functional theory, and EIS analysis uncovered the underlying mechanism of V<sup>3+</sup> substituted by Cr<sup>3+</sup> accounting for improving the electrochemistry performance. Our research made an important contribution to make the best of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>, bringing with excellent electrochemistry properties.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div                 10.1016/j.apsusc.2017.01.148</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D403%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D1-8%26journal%3DAppl.%2BSurf.%2BSci.%26author%3DC.%2BDing%26author%3DD.%2BSu%26author%3DW.%2BMa%26author%3DY.%2BZhao%26author%3DD.%2BYan%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BJin%26title%3DDesign%2Bof%2BHierarchical%2BCuS%252FGraphene%2BArchitectures%2Bwith%2BEnhanced%2BLithium%2BStorage%2BCapability%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.apsusc.2017.01.148&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c11975&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.apsusc.2017.01.148&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Design of hierarchical CuS/graphene architectures with enhanced lithium storage capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Caihua; Su, Dezhi; Ma, Wenxian; Zhao, Yongjie; Yan, Dong; Li, Jingbo; Jin, Haibo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">403</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1-8</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASUSEE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0169-4332</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-sulfides electrode materials usually suffer from poor cyclability and low rate capability in rechargeable batteries as a result of the pulverization of active materials and the loss of sulfur material induced by polysulfide dissoln.  Herein, we reported a delicate and scalable route for rational design of CuS/graphene composites.  Hierarchical CuS microparticles comprising of large amts. of self-assembled and well-arranged nanosheets uniformly mixed with flexible graphene layers.  The obtained CuS/graphene electrodes exhibited high specific capacities, excellent cycling stability and desirable rate capability when being evaluated as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries.  The high specific capacities of 568 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at 50 mA g-1 and 143 mA h g-1 at 1000 mA g-1 (in rate testing) were achieved, suggesting a very promising candidate for high-performance lithium-ion batteries.  The rationally designed structures of the CuS/graphene composites offered stable-hosts for Li+ insertion and alleviated the vol. changes upon cycling.  The presence of the graphene in composite not only constructed conductive paths and a network for fast transport of Li+, but also effectively reduced the dissoln. of polysulfides into electrolyte.  This graphene-based composite with hierarchical structure could be used as a safe, low-cost, and versatile material for extensively potential applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b00873"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Neat Design for the Structure of Electrode To Optimize the Lithium-Ion Battery Performance</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">27106</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">27115</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.8b00873</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D27106-27115%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DY.%2BZhao%26author%3DC.%2BDing%26author%3DY.%2BHao%26author%3DX.%2BZhai%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BJin%26title%3DNeat%2BDesign%2Bfor%2Bthe%2BStructure%2Bof%2BElectrode%2BTo%2BOptimize%2Bthe%2BLithium-Ion%2BBattery%2BPerformance%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.8b00873&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c11975&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.8b00873&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Neat Design for the Structure of Electrode To Optimize the Lithium-Ion Battery Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Yongjie; Ding, Caihua; Hao, Yanan; Zhai, Ximei; Wang, Chengzhi; Li, Yutao; Li, Jingbo; Jin, Haibo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">32</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">27106-27115</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The appearance of mech. cracks originated from anisotropic expansion and shrinkage of electrode particles during Li+ de/intercalation is a major cause of the capacity fading in Li-ion batteries.  Well-designed and controlled nanostructures of electrodes have shown a prominent prospect for solving this obstacle.  Here, a novel and convenient strategy is reported for the prepn. of graphene nanoscroll wrapping Nb2O5 nanoparticles (denoted as T-Nb2O5/G).  First, high energy ball milling is conducted to acquire softly agglomerated T-Nb2O5 nanoparticles owing to its spontaneous redn. of surface energy among these single particles.  Then freeze-drying leads to the formation of graphene nanoscroll, which easily realizes the in situ wrapping over softly agglomerated T-Nb2O5 nanoparticles.  Extended cycling tests demonstrate that such T-Nb2O5/G yields a high reversible specific capacity of 222 mA h g-1 over 700 cycles at 1C.  The dominated surface capacitive insertion processes possessing favorable kinetics enable T-Nb2O5/G to exhibit excellent rate performance, which achieve a capacity of 110 mA h g-1 at 10C.  A combined ex situ X-ray diffraction, SEM, and transmission electron microscopy investigation reveal that the long-term cycling stability of T-Nb2O5/G is attributed to the excellent structural stability of the electrode, in which the synergistic effect between the softly agglomerated T-Nb2O5 nanoparticles and graphene nanoscroll prevents the formation of mech. cracks.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201805381"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S. X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress of Layered Transition Metal Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1805381</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-Doping Stabilized High-Performance P2-Na0.66Li0.18Fe0.12Mn0.7O2 Cathode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Lufeng; Li, Xiang; Liu, Jue; Xiong, Shan; Ma, Xuetian; Liu, Pan; Bai, Jianming; Xu, Wenqian; Tang, Yuanzhi; Hu, Yan-Yan; Liu, Meilin; Chen, Hailong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">While sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold great promise for large-scale elec. energy storage and low speed elec. vehicles, the poor capacity retention of the cathode is one of the bottlenecks in the development of SIBs.  Following a strategy of using lithium doping in the transition-metal layer to stabilize the desodiated structure, a novel layered oxide cathode P2-Na0.66Li0.18Fe0.12Mn0.7O2 is designed and synthesized, which demonstrated a high capacity of 190 mAh g-1 and a remarkably high capacity retention of ∼87% after 80 cycles within a wide voltage range of 1.5-4.5 V.  The outstanding stability is attributed to the reversible migration of lithium during cycling and the elimination of the detrimental P2-O2 phase transition, revealed by ex situ and in situ X-ray diffraction and solid-state NMR spectroscopy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c6ee01750a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Clément, R.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structurally stable Mg-doped P2-Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 sodium-ion battery cathodes with high rate performance: insights from electrochemical, NMR and diffraction studies</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Clement, Raphaele J.; Billaud, Juliette; Robert Armstrong, A.; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo; Bruce, Peter G.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3240-3251</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are a more sustainable alternative to the existing lithium-ion technol. and could alleviate some of the stress on the global lithium market as a result of the growing elec. car and portable electronics industries.  Fundamental research focused on understanding the structural and electronic processes occurring on electrochem. cycling is key to devising rechargeable batteries with improved performance.  We present an in-depth investigation of the effect of Mg doping on the electrochem. performance and structural stability of Na2/3MnO2 with a P2 layer stacking by comparing three compns.: Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 (y = 0.0, 0.05, 0.1).  We show that Mg substitution leads to smoother electrochem., with fewer distinct electrochem. processes, improved rate performance and better capacity retention.  These observations are attributed to the more gradual structural changes upon charge and discharge, as obsd. with synchrotron, powder X-ray, and neutron diffraction.  Mg doping reduces the no. of Mn3+ Jahn-Teller centers and delays the high voltage phase transition occurring in P2-Na2/3MnO2.  The local structure is investigated using 23Na solid-state NMR (ssNMR) spectroscopy.  The ssNMR data provide direct evidence for fewer oxygen layer shearing events, leading to a stabilized P2 phase, and an enhanced Na-ion mobility up to 3.8 V vs.  Na+/Na upon Mg doping.  The 5% Mg-doped phase exhibits one of the best rate performances reported to date for sodium-ion cathodes with a P2 structure, with a reversible capacity of 106 mA h g-1 at the very high discharge rate of 5000 mA g-1.  In addn., its structure is highly reversible and stable cycling is obtained between 1.5 and 4.0 V vs.  Na+/Na, with a capacity of approx. 140 mA h g-1 retained after 50 cycles at a rate of 1000 mA g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7865"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, S.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Radially aligned hierarchical columnar structure as a cathode material for high energy density sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Oh, Seung-Min; Myung, Seung-Taek; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Belharouak, Ilias; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Delivery of high capacity with good retention is a challenge in developing cathodes for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.  Here we present a radially aligned hierarchical columnar structure in spherical particles with varied chem. compn. from the inner end (Na[Ni0.75Co0.02Mn0.23]O2) to the outer end (Na[Ni0.58Co0.06Mn0.36]O2) of the structure.  With this cathode material, we show that an electrochem. reaction based on Ni2+/3+/4+ is readily available to deliver a discharge capacity of 157 mAh (g-oxide)-1 (15 mA g-1), a capacity retention of 80% (125 mAh g-1) during 300 cycles in combination with a hard carbon anode, and a rate capability of 132.6 mAh g-1 (1,500 mA g-1, 10 C-rate).                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Na2/3Mn1-xAlxO2 cathode material for sodium-ion batteries: Al-doped enhanced electrochemical properties and studies on the electrode kinetics</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pang, Wei-Lin; Zhang, Xiao-Hua; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Li, Jin-Yue; Yan, Xin; Hou, Bao-Hua; Guan, Hong-Yu; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, Na-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as the promising alternative for Li-ion batteries.  Although layered P2-type transition metal oxides are an important class of cathode materials for SIBs, there are still some hurdles for the practical applications, including low specific capacity as well as poor cycling and rate properties.  Here, the electrochem. properties of layered Mn-based oxides were effectively improved via Al doping, which cannot only promote the formation of layered P2-type structure in the prepn. processes but also stabilize the lattice during the successive Na-intercalation/deintercalation due to suppression of the Jahn-Teller distortion of Mn3+.  Among the as-prepd. series of Na2/3Mn1-xAlxO2 (x =0, 1/18, 1/9, and 2/9), Na2/3Mn8/9Al1/9O2 with x =1/9 exhibits the optimal doping effect with the best electrochem. properties, in terms of the highest specific capacity of 162.3 mAh/g at 0.1 C, the highest rate capability, and the best cycling stability in comparison to the undoped Na2/3MnO2 and the other 2 materials with different Al-doped contents.  Both cyclic voltammetry at varied scan rates and galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique disclose the optimal electrode kinetics (the highest Na-diffusion coeff.) of the best Na2/3Mn8/9Al1/9O2.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b12204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, W.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, K.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.-P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>7/12</sub>Fe<sub>1/12</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material with Suppressed P2-O2 Phase Transition toward High-Performance Sodium-Ion Battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As a promising cathode material of sodium-ion battery, P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 (NNMO) possesses a theor. high capacity and working voltage to realize high energy storage d.  However, it still suffers from poor cycling stability mainly incurred by the undesirable P2-O2 phase transition.  Herein, the electrochem. active Fe3+ ions are introduced into the lattice of NNMO, forming Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3-xFexO2 (x = 0, 1/24, 1/12, 1/8, 1/6) to effectively stabilize the P2-type cryst. structure.  In such Fe-substituted materials, both Ni2+/Ni4+ and Fe3+/Fe4+ couples take part in the redox reactions, and the P2-O2 phase transition is well restrained during cycling, as verified by ex situ X-ray diffraction.  As a result, the optimized Na2/3Ni1/3Mn7/12Fe1/12O2 (1/12-NNMF) has a long-term cycling stability with the fading rate of 0.05% per cycle over 300 cycles at 5 C.  Furthermore, the 1/12-NNMF delivers excellent rate capabilities (65 mA h g-1 at 25 C) and superior low-temp. performance (the capacity retention of 94% at -25° after 80 cycles) owing to the enhanced Na diffusion upon Fe doping, which is deduced by the studies of electrode kinetics.  More significantly, the 1/12-NNMF also displays remarkable sodium-ion full-cell properties when merged with an LS-Sb@G anode, thus implying the possibility of their practical application.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701912"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Phase Transition, Air Stability, and Performance</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Al-doped Na3V2-xAlx(PO4)3/C (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.3) samples were synthesized and characterized as cathode materials for Na-ion batteries.  X-ray diffraction, electron microscopy and Raman spectroscopy data unveiled high purity NASICON phosphate phases and amorphous C forming a homogeneous matrix that enhances the electron conducting properties of the composite electrode material.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring Highly Reversible 1.5-Electron Reactions (V3+/V4+/V5+) in Na3VCr(PO4)3 Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Rui; Xu, Guiliang; Li, Qi; Zheng, Shiyao; Zheng, Guorui; Gong, Zhengliang; Li, Yixiao; Kruskop, Elizaveta; Fu, Riqiang; Chen, Zonghai; Amine, Khalil; Yang, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-coating-increased working voltage and energy density towards an advanced Na3V2(PO4)2F3@C cathode in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Zhen-Yi; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Sun, Zhong-Hui; Zhao, Xin-Xin; Li, Wen-Hao; Yang, Xu; Liang, Hao-Jie; Zhao, Chen-De; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science Bulletin</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">One main challenge for phosphate cathodes in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is to increase the working voltage and energy d. to promote its practicability.  Herein, an advanced Na3V2(PO4)2F3@C cathode is prepd. successfully for sodium-ion full cells.  It is revealed that, carbon coating can not only enhance the electronic cond. and electrode kinetics of Na3V2(PO4)2F3@C and inhibit the growth of particles (i.e., shorten the Na+-migration path), but also unexpectedly for the first time adjust the dis-/charging plateaux at different voltage ranges to increase the mean voltage (from 3.59 to 3.71 V) and energy d. (from 336.0 to 428.5 Wh kg-1) of phosphate cathode material.  As a result, when used as cathode for SIBs, the prepd. Na3V2(PO4)2F3@C delivers much improved electrochem. properties in terms of larger specifc capacity (115.9 vs. 93.5 mAh g-1), more outstanding high-rate capability (e.g., 87.3 vs. 60.5 mAh g-1 at 10 C), higher energy d., and better cycling performance, compared to pristine Na3V2(PO4)2F3.  Reasons for the enhanced electrochem. properties include ionicity enhancement of lattice induced by carbon coating, improved electrode kinetics and electronic cond., and high stability of lattice, which is elucidated clearly through the contrastive characterization and electrochem. studies.  Moreover, excellent energy-storage performance in sodium-ion full cells further demonstrate the extremely high possibility of Na3V2(PO4)2F3@C cathode for practical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201907645"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M. Isostructural and multivalent anion substitution combined with carbon coating is proposed to greatly improve the electrochem. properties of phosphate cathode in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Specifically, multivalent tetrahedral SiO44- substitute for PO43- in Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) lattice, prepg. the optimal Na3.1V2(PO4)2.9(SiO4)0.1 with high-rate capability (delivering a high capacity of 82.5 mAh g-1 even at 20 C) and outstanding cyclic stability (≈98% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 1 C).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolytes are essential for the proper functioning of any battery technol. and the emerging Na-ion technol. is no exception.  Hence, a major focus on battery research is to identify the most appropriate formulation so as to minimize interface reactions and enhance both cell performances and safety aspects.  In order to identify suitable electrolyte formulations for Na-ion chem. we benchmarked various electrolytes contg. diverse solvent mixts. (cyclic, acyclic carbonates, glymes) and Na-based salts having either F-based or perchlorate anions and measured viscosity, ionic cond., and thermal and electrochem. stability. (GICs).  Based on the computational results, the authors propose a dual-interface model to describe the delithiation mechanism of LiFePO4 upon charging.  Accordingly, the exptl. obsd. LiFePO4/stage-II/FePO4 3-phase coexistence could be successfully reproduced.  Formation of Li-staging configuration is mainly attributed to the Fe center mediated interlayer Li-Li interactions, which is an essential indirect electrostatic force. The most favorable intrinsic defect type is found to be the cation antisite defect, in which Li and M ions exchange positions.  Li migration is found to occur preferentially down [010] channels, following a curved trajectory.  Defect assocn. or binding energies for pair clusters composed of combinations of lithium vacancies, antisite cations, and small polaron species are investigated.  Migration energies for divalent antisite cations on Li sites suggest that such defects would impede Li diffusion in LiMPO4 to varying degrees.  Calcn. of dopant substitution energies for cations with charges +1 to +5 indicate that supervalent doping (e.g., Ga3+, Ti4+, Nb5+) on either Li or M sites is energetically unfavorable and does not result in a large increase in electronic (small polaron) species.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1021/cm050999v"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M. The most favorable intrinsic defect is the Li-Fe anti-site pair in which a Li-ion (on the M1 site) and an Fe-ion (on the M2 site) are interchanged.
